\[ \log(\lambda_i) = \eta_i = \mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta} = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_{i1} + \dots + \beta_k x_{ik} \]

\subsection{Canonical Representation}\label{canonical-representation}

We begin by expressing the log-likelihood in the canonical exponential
family form. The probability mass function for the Poisson distribution
is \(P(Y_i=y_i) = \frac{e^{-\lambda_i}\lambda_i^{y_i}}{y_i!}\). The
log-likelihood is:
\[ \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \left( y_i \log(\lambda_i) - \lambda_i - \log(y_i!) \right) \]

Substituting the log link
\(\log(\lambda_i) = \mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}\) and
\(\lambda_i = e^{\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}}\):
\[ \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \left( y_i (\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) - e^{\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}} \right) + \text{const} \]
Rearranging terms to isolate the parameters \(\beta_j\):
\[ \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}; \mathbf{y}) = \sum_{j=0}^k \beta_j \underbrace{\left( \sum_{i=1}^n y_i x_{ij} \right)}_{T_j(\mathbf{y})} - \underbrace{\sum_{i=1}^n e^{\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}}}_{A(\boldsymbol{\beta})} + \text{const} \]

From this form, we identify:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Sufficient Statistics:}
  \(\mathbf{T}(\mathbf{y}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{y}\).
\item
  \textbf{Log-Partition Function:}
  \(A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i = \sum_{i=1}^n e^{\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}}\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{The Score and Information}\label{the-score-and-information}

Next, we derive the gradient and Hessian of the log-likelihood.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Score Vector (\(\mathbf{U}\)):} The gradient of
  \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\beta})\) is the difference between the observed
  sufficient statistics and their expectations (derived from
  \(\nabla A\)).
  \[ \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\beta}} \ell = \mathbf{T}(\mathbf{y}) - \nabla A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n y_i \mathbf{x}_i - \sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i \mathbf{x}_i = \mathbf{X}^\top (\mathbf{y} - \boldsymbol{\lambda}) \]
\item
  \textbf{Fisher Information Matrix (\(\mathcal{J}\)):} This is the
  negative Hessian of the log-likelihood, or equivalently the Hessian of
  the log-partition function \(A(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
  \[ \mathcal{J}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \nabla^2 A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \mathbf{x}_i \frac{\partial \lambda_i}{\partial \boldsymbol{\beta}^\top} = \sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i \mathbf{x}_i \mathbf{x}_i^\top \]
  In matrix notation,
  \(\mathcal{J}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{W} \mathbf{X}\),
  where \(\mathbf{W} = \text{diag}(\lambda_i)\). Note that for the
  Poisson model, the variance equals the mean \(\lambda_i\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Asymptotic Distributions
(Theory)}\label{asymptotic-distributions-theory}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\alph{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Normality of the Score:} The Score vector
  \(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n (Y_i - \lambda_i)\mathbf{x}_i\)
  is a sum of independent mean-zero random vectors.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Mean: \(E[\mathbf{U}] = \mathbf{0}\).
  \item
    Variance:
    \(\text{Var}(\mathbf{U}) = \sum \text{Var}(Y_i)\mathbf{x}_i\mathbf{x}_i^\top = \mathcal{J}(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
    By the \textbf{Multivariate Central Limit Theorem}, as
    \(n \to \infty\):
    \[ \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}}\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) \xrightarrow{d} N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\beta})) \]
    where \(\mathcal{I}\) is the limit of \(\frac{1}{n}\mathcal{J}\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Normality of the Estimator:} The MLE
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}\) satisfies
  \(\mathbf{U}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}) = \mathbf{0}\). Taking a
  first-order Taylor expansion around the true parameter
  \(\boldsymbol{\beta}\):
  \[ \mathbf{0} = \mathbf{U}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}) \approx \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) - \mathcal{J}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} - \boldsymbol{\beta}) \]
  Rearranging gives
  \((\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} - \boldsymbol{\beta}) \approx \mathcal{J}^{-1}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
  Since \(\mathbf{U}\) is asymptotically normal, the linear
  transformation implies:
  \[ \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} - \boldsymbol{\beta}) \xrightarrow{d} N\left(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}^{-1}(\boldsymbol{\beta})\right) \]
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Numerical Application in
R}\label{numerical-application-in-r}

We now implement the Newton-Raphson algorithm in R to estimate
parameters for a Poisson regression model with a \textbf{continuous
covariate}. Let \(\log(\lambda_i) = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_i\).

\textbf{a. Data Generation} We simulate \(n=100\) observations using a
continuous predictor \(x\) drawn from a Uniform distribution.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{123}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{100}
\NormalTok{x }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{runif}\NormalTok{(n, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# True parameters: Intercept=0.5, Slope=2.0}
\NormalTok{beta\_true }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{2.0}\NormalTok{) }

\CommentTok{\# Generate response Y}
\NormalTok{lambda\_true }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{exp}\NormalTok{(beta\_true[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\SpecialCharTok{+}\NormalTok{ beta\_true[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{] }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ x)}
\NormalTok{y }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rpois}\NormalTok{(n, lambda\_true)}

\CommentTok{\# Design Matrix (intercept column + covariate)}
\NormalTok{X }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{cbind}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, x)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{b. Newton-Raphson Implementation} The update rule is
\(\boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)} + \mathcal{J}^{-1}\mathbf{U}\).
We implement this iteratively.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{newton\_raphson\_poisson }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \ControlFlowTok{function}\NormalTok{(X, y, }\AttributeTok{tol =} \FloatTok{1e{-}6}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{max\_iter =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{) \{}
\NormalTok{  beta }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{rep}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\FunctionTok{ncol}\NormalTok{(X)) }\CommentTok{\# Start at 0}
  
  \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ (i }\ControlFlowTok{in} \DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\NormalTok{max\_iter) \{}
    \CommentTok{\# 1. Compute means and weights}
\NormalTok{    eta }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ X }\SpecialCharTok{\%*\%}\NormalTok{ beta}
\NormalTok{    lambda }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{as.vector}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{exp}\NormalTok{(eta))}
    
    \CommentTok{\# 2. Compute Score U and Info J}
\NormalTok{    U }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{crossprod}\NormalTok{(X, y }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ lambda)}
\NormalTok{    J }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{crossprod}\NormalTok{(X }\SpecialCharTok{*}\NormalTok{ lambda, X) }\CommentTok{\# Efficient t(X) \%*\% W \%*\% X}
    
    \CommentTok{\# 3. Update beta}
\NormalTok{    delta }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{solve}\NormalTok{(J, U)}
\NormalTok{    beta\_new }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ beta }\SpecialCharTok{+} \FunctionTok{as.vector}\NormalTok{(delta)}
    
    \CommentTok{\# 4. Check convergence}
\NormalTok{    diff }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{((beta\_new }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ beta)}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
    \FunctionTok{cat}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{sprintf}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Iter \%d: beta0=\%.4f, beta1=\%.4f, diff=\%.6f}\SpecialCharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{                i, beta\_new[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], beta\_new[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{], diff))}
    
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (diff }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{ tol) }\FunctionTok{return}\NormalTok{(beta\_new)}
\NormalTok{    beta }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ beta\_new}
\NormalTok{  \}}
\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{beta\_mle }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{newton\_raphson\_poisson}\NormalTok{(X, y)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Output:}

\begin{verbatim}
Iter 1: beta0=1.1718, beta1=0.6234, diff=1.761592
Iter 2: beta0=0.6970, beta1=1.5794, diff=1.139433
Iter 3: beta0=0.4996, beta1=2.0101, diff=0.224446
Iter 4: beta0=0.4725, beta1=2.0838, diff=0.006173
Iter 5: beta0=0.4722, beta1=2.0845, diff=0.000001
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{c.~Discussion \& Verification} We compare our manual estimates
with R's built-in \texttt{glm} function.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cat}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Manual MLE: "}\NormalTok{, beta\_mle, }\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{cat}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"GLM Output: "}\NormalTok{, }\FunctionTok{coef}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{glm}\NormalTok{(y }\SpecialCharTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ x, }\AttributeTok{family =} \StringTok{"poisson"}\NormalTok{)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Observation:} The manual implementation converges to the exact
same values as the built-in function
(\(\hat{\beta}_0 \approx 0.47, \hat{\beta}_1 \approx 2.08\)). Notice
that we started at \(\boldsymbol{\beta}=(0,0)\). In the first iteration,
the algorithm took a large step because the log-likelihood surface is
steep. The quadratic convergence of Newton-Raphson is evident in the
rapid decrease of the difference term (from 1.13 to 0.22 to 0.006).

\bookmarksetup{startatroot}

\chapter{Minimum Variance Estimators}\label{minimum-variance-estimators}

\section{Completeness}\label{completeness}

\begin{definition}[Complete
Statistic]\protect\hypertarget{def-completeness}{}\label{def-completeness}

A statistic \(T\) is said to be \textbf{complete} if for any real-valued
function \(g\), \[
E[g(T)|\theta] = 0 \quad \text{for all } \theta
\] implies \[
P(g(T) = 0 | \theta) = 1 \quad \text{for all } \theta
\]

\end{definition}

Significance: If \(T\) is complete, then there exists at most one
unbiased estimator for \(\theta\) that is a function of \(T\).

\begin{example}[Uniform Distribution (Not
Complete)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-uniform-incomplete}{}\label{exm-uniform-incomplete}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \sim \text{Unif}(\theta-1, \theta+1)\). The
density is:

\[
f(x) = \prod I(\theta-1 < x_i < \theta+1) = I(\theta \in (x_{(n)}-1, x_{(1)}+1))
\]

The statistic \(T(X) = (X_{(1)}, X_{(n)})\) is a Minimal Sufficient
Statistic. However, it is \textbf{not complete}.

Consider the range \(R = X_{(n)} - X_{(1)}\). The distribution of \(R\)
does not depend on \(\theta\) (it is an ancillary statistic). Let
\(g(T) = X_{(n)} - X_{(1)} - c\), where \(c = E[X_{(n)} - X_{(1)}]\).
Then \(E[g(T)] = 0\) for all \(\theta\), but \(g(T)\) is not identically
zero.

\end{example}

\begin{lemma}[Exponential Family
Completeness]\protect\hypertarget{lem-exponential-complete}{}\label{lem-exponential-complete}

If \(T = (T_1, \dots, T_k)\) is the natural statistic of an exponential
family that contains an open rectangle in the parameter space, then
\(T\) is complete.

\end{lemma}

\section{UMVUE}\label{umvue}

\begin{definition}[Uniformly Minimum Variance Unbiased Estimator
(UMVUE)]\protect\hypertarget{def-umvue}{}\label{def-umvue}

A statistic \(T(x)\) is a UMVUE for \(\theta\) if:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(E(T(x)|\theta) = \theta\) for all \(\theta\) (Unbiased).
\item
  \(Var(T(x)|\theta) \le Var(d(x)|\theta)\) for all \(\theta\) and for
  all other unbiased estimators \(d(x)\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

The relationship between statistics types is visualized below:

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{umvue_files/figure-pdf/fig-stats-hierarchy-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-stats-hierarchy}Hierarchy of Statistics}

\end{figure}%

\begin{theorem}[Lehmann-Scheffe
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-lehmann-scheffe}{}\label{thm-lehmann-scheffe}

If \(T\) is a complete and sufficient statistic, and there is an
unbiased estimator \(d(X)\) such that \(E[d(X)] = \theta\), then
\(\phi(T) = E[d(X)|T]\) is the unique UMVUE for \(\theta\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Rao-Blackwell
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-rao-blackwell}{}\label{thm-rao-blackwell}

Given that \(T\) is a sufficient statistic and \(d_1(x)\) is an unbiased
estimator (\(E[d_1(x)] = \theta\)). Define \(g(T) = E[d_1(x) | T]\).
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(g(T)\) is a statistic (free of \(\theta\) because \(T\) is
  sufficient).
\item
  \(E[g(T)] = \theta\) (Unbiased).
\item
  \(Var(g(T)) \le Var(d_1(x))\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Proof of Rao-Blackwell:}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Since \(T\) is sufficient, the conditional distribution \(X|T\) is
  independent of \(\theta\), so \(g(T)\) is a valid statistic.
\item
  By the Law of Iterated Expectations: \[
      E[g(T)] = E_T[ E_X(d_1(X)|T) ] = E_X[d_1(X)] = \theta
      \]
\item
  By the variance decomposition formula: \[
  Var(d_1(X)) = Var(E[d_1(X)|T]) + E[Var(d_1(X)|T)]
  \] \[
  Var(d_1(X)) = Var(g(T)) + E[(d_1(X) - g(T))^2 | T]
  \] Since \((d_1(X) - g(T))^2 \ge 0\), we have
  \(Var(g(T)) \le Var(d_1(X))\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\section{Methods for Finding UMVUE}\label{methods-for-finding-umvue}

To find the UMVUE for a parameter \(\theta\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Find a Complete Sufficient Statistic:} Identify \(T\) which is
  complete and sufficient for \(\theta\) (often using the Exponential
  Family properties).
\item
  \textbf{Find an Unbiased Estimator:} Find any simple statistic
  \(d(X)\) such that \(E[d(X)] = \theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Rao-Blackwellize:} Compute \(g(T) = E[d(X)|T]\). The result
  \(g(T)\) is the UMVUE.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{example}[Poisson
UMVUE]\protect\hypertarget{exm-poisson-umvue}{}\label{exm-poisson-umvue}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \sim \text{Poisson}(\lambda)\). Find the UMVUE for
\(\lambda\) and \(\lambda^2\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  For \(\lambda\): \(T = \sum X_i\) is a complete sufficient statistic
  (Poisson is exponential family). Let \(d_1(X) = X_1\).
  \(E[X_1] = \lambda\). We compute \(g(T) = E[X_1 | T]\). Since the
  conditional distribution of \(X_1\) given \(T=t\) is
  Binomial(\(t, 1/n\)):
\end{enumerate}

\[
E[X_1 | T] = t \cdot \frac{1}{n} = \frac{T}{n} = \bar{X}
\]

Thus, \(\bar{X}\) is the UMVUE for \(\lambda\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  For \(\lambda^2\): We know
  \(Var(X_1) = \lambda = E(X_1^2) - (E(X_1))^2\). So
  \(E(X_1^2) - \lambda = \lambda^2\), which implies
  \(E(X_1^2 - X_1) = \lambda^2\). Let \(d_2(X) = X_1^2 - X_1\). This is
  an unbiased estimator for \(\lambda^2\).
\end{enumerate}

We calculate \(g(T) = E[X_1^2 - X_1 | T]\).

\[
g(T) = E[X_1^2 | T] - E[X_1 | T]
\]

Using the second moment of the Binomial distribution \(Bin(T, 1/n)\):
\(E[X_1^2|T] = Var(X_1|T) + (E[X_1|T])^2 = T \frac{1}{n}(1-\frac{1}{n}) + (\frac{T}{n})^2\).

\[
g(T) = \left[ \frac{T}{n} - \frac{T}{n^2} + \frac{T^2}{n^2} \right] - \frac{T}{n} = \frac{T^2 - T}{n^2} = \frac{T(T-1)}{n^2}
\]

Thus, \(\frac{T(T-1)}{n^2}\) is the UMVUE for \(\lambda^2\).

\end{example}

\bookmarksetup{startatroot}

\chapter{Hypothesis Testing}\label{hypothesis-testing-1}

\section{General Terminologies}\label{general-terminologies}

\subsection{Hypothesis Testing}\label{hypothesis-testing-2}

We formulate the problem of hypothesis testing as deciding between two
competing claims about a parameter \(\theta\):

\[
H_0: \theta \in \Theta_0 \quad \text{(Null Hypothesis)}
\]

\[
H_1: \theta \in \Theta_1 \quad \text{(Alternative Hypothesis)}
\]

\begin{definition}[Simple and Composite
Hypotheses]\protect\hypertarget{def-simple-composite}{}\label{def-simple-composite}

A hypothesis is called \textbf{simple} if it specifies a single value
for the parameter (e.g., \(\Theta_0\) contains only one point). It is
called \textbf{composite} if it specifies more than one value.

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[Normal Mean
Test]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-hypotheses}{}\label{exm-normal-hypotheses}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\mu, \sigma^2)\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(\sigma^2\) is known, \(H_0: \mu = \mu_0\) is a simple hypothesis.
\item
  If \(\sigma^2\) is unknown, \(H_0: \mu = \mu_0\) is a composite
  hypothesis (since \(\sigma^2\) can vary).
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\subsection{Test Functions and Size}\label{test-functions-and-size}

A test is defined by a \textbf{critical region} \(C_\alpha\) such that
we reject \(H_0\) if the data \(x \in C_\alpha\). Equivalently, we can
define a \textbf{test function} \(\phi(x)\) representing the probability
of rejecting \(H_0\) given data \(x\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A non-randomized test is given as follows:
\end{itemize}

\[
\phi(x) = I(x \in C_\alpha) = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } x \in C_\alpha \text{ (Reject } H_0 \text{)} \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A randomized test, \(\phi(x)\) can take values in \([0, 1]\), which
  can be expressed typically as follows:

  \[
   \phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
   1 & \text{if } x \in C_1 \\
   \gamma & \text{if } x \in C_* \\
   0 & \text{otherwise}
   \end{cases}
   \]

  where:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(C_1\) is the region where we strictly reject \(H_0\).
  \item
    \(C_*\) is the boundary region (often where \(T(x) = k\)) where we
    reject \(H_0\) with probability \(\gamma\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  More generally, \(\phi(x)\) is just a function of \(x\) with values in
  \([0,1]\), which represents the probability that we will reject
  \(H_0\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{example}[Randomized Test for
Binomial]\protect\hypertarget{exm-randomized-test}{}\label{exm-randomized-test}

Let \(X \sim \text{Bin}(n=10, \theta)\). Consider testing
\(H_0: \theta = 1/2\) vs \(H_1: \theta > 1/2\) with target size
\(\alpha = 0.05\).

Suppose we choose a critical region \(X \ge k\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(k=9\), \(P(X \ge 9 | \theta=0.5) \approx 0.0107\).
\item
  If \(k=8\), \(P(X \ge 8 | \theta=0.5) \approx 0.0547\).
\end{itemize}

Since we cannot achieve exactly 0.05 with a non-randomized test (the
survival function jumps over 0.05), we must use a randomized test
function.

The randomized test is defined as:

\[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } x \in C_1 \text{ (i.e., } x \ge 9) \\
\gamma & \text{if } x \in C_* \text{ (i.e., } x = 8) \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

From the figure, we see that \(\alpha = 0.05\) lies between
\(P(X \ge 9)\) and \(P(X \ge 8)\). We always reject the ``tail'' where
probabilities are strictly less than \(\alpha\) (here \(x \ge 9\)). At
the boundary \(x=8\), we cannot reject with probability 1 (which would
give total size 0.0547), nor with probability 0 (which would give total
size 0.0107).

We choose \(\gamma\) to bridge this gap:

\[
\begin{aligned}
\alpha &= P(X \ge 9) + \gamma \cdot P(X = 8) \\
0.05 &= 0.01074 + \gamma \cdot (P(X \ge 8) - P(X \ge 9)) \\
0.05 &= 0.01074 + \gamma \cdot (0.05469 - 0.01074)
\end{aligned}
\]

Solving for \(\gamma\):

\[
\gamma = \frac{0.05 - 0.01074}{0.04395} \approx \frac{39}{44} \approx 0.89
\]

\end{example}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-binomial-survival-zoom-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-binomial-survival-zoom}Survival Function P(X
\textgreater= k) with randomization detail}

\end{figure}%

\section{Power and Size Function}\label{power-and-size-function}

\begin{definition}[Size of a
Test]\protect\hypertarget{def-test-size}{}\label{def-test-size}

The \textbf{size} of a test \(\phi(x)\) is the maximum probability of
rejecting the null hypothesis when it is true:

\[
\text{Size}(\phi) = \sup_{\theta \in \Theta_0} W_\phi(\theta) = \sup_{\theta \in \Theta_0} E_\theta[\phi(X)]
\]

\end{definition}

\subsection{Power Definitions}\label{power-definitions}

We distinguish between the power function varying over parameters and
the power metric of a specific test.

\textbf{1. Power Function (\(W_\phi(\theta)\))} The probability of
rejecting \(H_0\) as a function of the parameter \(\theta\):

\[
W_\phi(\theta) = E_\theta[\phi(X)]
\]

\textbf{2. Power of the Test (\(\text{Power}(\phi)\))} In the context of
a specific alternative hypothesis (e.g., \(H_1: \theta = \theta_1\)), we
define the power as a scalar functional of \(\phi\):

\[
\text{Power}(\phi) = E_{\theta_1}[\phi(X)]
\]

Ideally, we want:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(W_\phi(\theta) \le \text{Size}(\phi)\) for all
  \(\theta \in \Theta_0\) (Control Type I error).
\item
  \(\text{Power}(\phi)\) to be as large as possible (Maximize
  sensitivity to \(H_1\)).
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-test-function-size-power-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-test-function-size-power}Illustration of a Test
Function \(\phi(x)\) (Pink) relative to Size (\(H_0\), Blue) and Power
(\(H_1\), Red).}

\end{figure}%

\section{The Neyman-Pearson Lemma}\label{the-neyman-pearson-lemma}

Consider testing a simple null against a simple alternative:
\(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta = \theta_1\).

We define the \textbf{Likelihood Ratio} \(\Lambda(x)\) as:

\[
\Lambda(x) = \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} = \frac{f(x; \theta_1)}{f(x; \theta_0)}
\]

\begin{definition}[Likelihood Ratio Test
(LRT)]\protect\hypertarget{def-lrt}{}\label{def-lrt}

A test \(\phi(x)\) is a Likelihood Ratio Test if it has the form:

\[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } \Lambda(x) > k \\
\gamma(x) & \text{if } \Lambda(x) = k \\
0 & \text{if } \Lambda(x) < k
\end{cases}
\]

where \(k \ge 0\) is a constant and \(0 \le \gamma(x) \le 1\).

\end{definition}

\subsection{Neyman-Pearson Lemma}\label{neyman-pearson-lemma}

\begin{theorem}[Neyman-Pearson
Lemma]\protect\hypertarget{thm-neyman-pearson}{}\label{thm-neyman-pearson}

~

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\alph{enumi})}
\item
  \textbf{Optimality:} For any \(k\) and \(\gamma(x)\), the LRT
  \(\phi_0(x)\) defined above has maximum power among all tests whose
  size is less than or equal to the size of \(\phi_0(x)\).
\item
  \textbf{Existence:} Given \(\alpha \in (0, 1)\), there exist constants
  \(k\) and \(\gamma_0\) such that the LRT defined by this \(k\) and
  \(\gamma(x) = \gamma_0\) has size exactly \(\alpha\).
\item
  \textbf{Uniqueness:} If a test \(\phi\) has size \(\alpha\) and is of
  maximum power among all tests of size \(\alpha\), then \(\phi\) is
  necessarily an LRT, except possibly on a set of measure zero under
  \(H_0\) and \(H_1\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\subsection{A Derivation with The Lagrange Multiplier
Approach}\label{a-derivation-with-the-lagrange-multiplier-approach}

To make the optimality of the Likelihood Ratio Test (LRT) intuitive, we
can frame the search for the best test function \(\phi(x)\) as a
constrained optimization problem.

We want to maximize the power of the test:

\[
\text{Power}(\phi) = \int \phi(x) f_1(x) dx
\]

subject to the constraint on the size of the test \(\alpha\):

\[
\text{Size}(\phi) = \int \phi(x) f_0(x) dx = \alpha
\]

Using the method of Lagrange multipliers, we define the objective
function \(L\) with a multiplier \(k\):

\[
L(\phi, k) = \int \phi(x) f_1(x) dx - k \left( \int \phi(x) f_0(x) dx - \alpha \right)
\]

Rearranging the terms inside the integral, we get:

\[
L(\phi, k) = \int \phi(x) [f_1(x) - k f_0(x)] dx + k\alpha
\]

To maximize \(L\) with respect to \(\phi(x)\), we look at the integrand.
Since \(0 \le \phi(x) \le 1\), we should choose \(\phi(x)\) to be as
large as possible whenever its coefficient is positive, and as small as
possible whenever its coefficient is negative:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) > 0\), set \(\phi(x) = 1\).
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) < 0\), set \(\phi(x) = 0\).
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) = 0\), the value of \(\phi(x)\) does not affect
  the integral (this is where \(\gamma\) comes in).
\end{itemize}

This decision rule is equivalent to:

\[
\phi(x) = 
\begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} > k \\
0 & \text{if } \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} < k
\end{cases}
\]

This is precisely the form of the Likelihood Ratio Test. The ``shadow
price'' or Lagrange multiplier \(k\) represents the critical threshold
that balances the gain in power against the cost of increasing the Type
I error.

\subsection{Proof of NP Lemma}\label{proof-of-np-lemma}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Proof of (a) Optimality:} Let \(\phi_{LRT}\) be the LRT with
size \(\alpha\), and \(\phi\) be any other test with size
\(\le \alpha\). Define the function \(U(x)\) as the difference in test
functions weighted by the linear combination of densities:

\[
U(x) = (\phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x))
\]

We analyze the sign of \(U(x)\) by looking at the sign of its two
factors in three cases:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) > 0\) (implies \(\Lambda(x) > k\)). Since
  \(\phi_{LRT}(x) = 1\) and \(\phi(x) \le 1\), we have:

  \[
    \begin{aligned}
    \phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x) &\ge 0 \\
    U(x) = (\phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) &\ge 0
    \end{aligned}
    \]
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) < 0\) (implies \(\Lambda(x) < k\)). Since
  \(\phi_{LRT}(x) = 0\) and \(\phi(x) \ge 0\), we have:

  \[
    \begin{aligned}
    \phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x) &\le 0 \\
    U(x) = (\phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) &\ge 0
    \end{aligned}
    \]
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) = 0\). The product is zero regardless of the
  test functions.

  \[
    U(x) = 0
    \]
\end{itemize}

Combining these cases, we conclude that the product is non-negative for
all \(x\):

\[
U(x) = (\phi_{LRT}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) \ge 0
\]

Therefore, integrating \(U(x)\) over the entire domain:

\[
\int U(x) dx = \int (\phi_{LRT} - \phi)(f_1 - k f_0) dx \ge 0
\]

Expanding the integral:

\[
\int \phi_{LRT} f_1 - \int \phi f_1 - k \left( \int \phi_{LRT} f_0 - \int \phi f_0 \right) \ge 0
\]

Converting to expectations:

\[
E_{\theta_1}[\phi_{LRT}] - E_{\theta_1}[\phi] - k (E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{LRT}] - E_{\theta_0}[\phi]) \ge 0
\]

Since \(E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{LRT}] = \alpha\) and
\(E_{\theta_0}[\phi] \le \alpha\), the difference
\((E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{LRT}] - E_{\theta_0}[\phi]) \ge 0\). Given that
\(k \ge 0\):

\[
E_{\theta_1}[\phi_{LRT}] - E_{\theta_1}[\phi] \ge 0 \implies \text{Power}(\phi_{LRT}) \ge \text{Power}(\phi)
\]

\textbf{Proof of (b) Existence:} Let
\(G(k) = P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) \le k)\). \(G(k)\) is the cumulative
distribution function of the random variable \(\Lambda(X)\), so it is
non-decreasing. We seek \(k_0\) such that \(1 - G(k_0) \approx \alpha\).
Because of discrete jumps, we might not hit \(\alpha\) exactly. We
choose \(k_0\) such that:

\[
P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) > k_0) \le \alpha \le P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) \ge k_0)
\]

Set
\(\gamma_0 = \frac{\alpha - P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) > k_0)}{P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) = k_0)}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-lrt-proof-transparent-pink-fixed-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-lrt-proof-transparent-pink-fixed}Visualizing the NP
Lemma: The thick, transparent pink line is \(\phi_{LRT}\). The thin
solid black line is \(\phi\). Overlap is visible as a black line inside
pink.}

\end{figure}%

\section{Uniformly Most Powerful (UMP)
Tests}\label{uniformly-most-powerful-ump-tests}

When the alternative hypothesis is composite
(\(H_1: \theta \in \Theta_1\)), we seek a test that is ``best'' for
\emph{all} \(\theta \in \Theta_1\).

\begin{definition}[Uniformly Most Powerful
Test]\protect\hypertarget{def-ump}{}\label{def-ump}

A test \(\phi_0(x)\) of size \(\alpha\) is \textbf{Uniformly Most
Powerful (UMP)} if:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(E_{\theta}[\phi_0(X)] \le \alpha\) for all \(\theta \in \Theta_0\).
\item
  For any other test \(\phi(x)\) satisfying (1),
  \(E_{\theta}[\phi_0(X)] \ge E_{\theta}[\phi(X)]\) for all
  \(\theta \in \Theta_1\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-ump-power-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-ump-power}Power functions of UMP test vs.~another
test}

\end{figure}%

\section{Monotone Likelihood Ratio
(MLR)}\label{monotone-likelihood-ratio-mlr}

\begin{definition}[Monotone Likelihood
Ratio]\protect\hypertarget{def-mlr}{}\label{def-mlr}

A family of densities \(\{f(x; \theta)\}\) has a \textbf{Monotone
Likelihood Ratio (MLR)} with respect to a statistic \(T(x)\) if for any
\(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the ratio:

\[
\frac{f(x; \theta_1)}{f(x; \theta_0)}
\]

is a non-decreasing function of \(T(x)\).

\end{definition}

Common examples include the one-parameter Exponential Family:
\(f(x; \theta) = h(x) c(\theta) \exp\{w(\theta) T(x)\}\). If
\(w(\theta)\) is increasing, the family has MLR w.r.t \(T(x)\).

\subsection*{Karlin-Rubin Theorem}\label{karlin-rubin-theorem}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Karlin-Rubin Theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Karlin-Rubin
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-karlin-rubin}{}\label{thm-karlin-rubin}

Suppose \(X\) has a distribution from a family with MLR with respect to
\(T(X)\), and the distribution of \(T(X)\) is continuous. Consider
testing \(H_0: \theta \le \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta > \theta_0\).

The test:

\[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } T(x) > t_0 \\
0 & \text{if } T(x) \le t_0
\end{cases}
\]

where \(t_0\) is determined by \(P_{\theta_0}(T(X) > t_0) = \alpha\), is
the UMP size \(\alpha\) test.

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof of Theorem 4.2 (UMP for MLR Families)]
\textbf{The Test:} Define the test \(\phi(x)\) as: \[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } T(x) > t_0 \\
0 & \text{if } T(x) \le t_0
\end{cases}
\] where \(t_0\) is determined such that the power at the boundary is
\(\alpha\), i.e., \(W_{LR}(\theta_0) = \alpha\).

\textbf{(1) Application of Neyman-Pearson Lemma} Because the family has
a Monotone Likelihood Ratio (MLR) in \(T(x)\), for any specific
alternative \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the likelihood ratio \(\Lambda(x)\)
is an increasing function of \(T(x)\). Therefore, the rejection region
\(T(x) > t_0\) corresponds to \(\Lambda(x) > k\). By the Neyman-Pearson
Lemma, \(\phi(x)\) is the Most Powerful (MP) test for testing
\(H_0': \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1': \theta = \theta_1\).

\textbf{(2) Monotonicity of the Power Function} We claim that
\(W_{LR}(\theta)\) is a non-decreasing function of \(\theta\).

\emph{Proof:} Let \(\theta_2 < \theta_1\). Consider testing
\(\theta = \theta_2\) vs \(\theta = \theta_1\). Let
\(\beta = W_{LR}(\theta_2)\). Define a constant dummy test
\(\phi^*(x) = \beta\) for all \(x\). The power of this test is constant:
\(W_{\phi^*}(\theta) = \beta\). Since \(\phi(x)\) corresponds to the
likelihood ratio test form (reject for large \(T\)) for \(\theta_2\) vs
\(\theta_1\), it is the MP test of size \(\beta\). By the optimality of
the NP Lemma, the power of \(\phi\) at \(\theta_1\) must be at least the
power of the competitor \(\phi^*\): \[
W_{LR}(\theta_1) \ge W_{\phi^*}(\theta_1) = \beta = W_{LR}(\theta_2)
\] Thus, \(W_{LR}(\theta)\) is non-decreasing.

\textbf{(3) Size Control} Since \(W_{LR}(\theta)\) is non-decreasing and
we set \(W_{LR}(\theta_0) = \alpha\): \[
W_{LR}(\theta) \le W_{LR}(\theta_0) = \alpha \quad \text{for all } \theta \le \theta_0
\] This proves the test satisfies the size constraint for the composite
null \(H_0: \theta \le \theta_0\).

\textbf{(4) UMP Property} Let \(\phi'(x)\) be any other test with size
\(\le \alpha\) for \(H_0: \theta \le \theta_0\). This implies
\(W_{\phi'}(\theta_0) \le \alpha\). For any specific
\(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), we treat the problem as testing \(\theta_0\) vs
\(\theta_1\). Since \(\phi(x)\) is the MP test for \(\theta_0\) vs
\(\theta_1\) (from Step 1), and \(\phi'\) is a valid competitor (size
\(\le \alpha\) at \(\theta_0\)), we have: \[
W_{LR}(\theta_1) \ge W_{\phi'}(\theta_1)
\] Since this holds for all \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), \(\phi(x)\) is the
Uniformly Most Powerful (UMP) test.
\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-mlr-dist-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-mlr-dist}Distribution of statistic T under H0 and H1
with MLR}

\end{figure}%

\begin{example}[UMP Test for
Exponential/Gamma]\protect\hypertarget{exm-ump-gamma}{}\label{exm-ump-gamma}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} \text{Exp}(\theta)\) with pdf
\(f(x) = \frac{1}{\theta} e^{-x/\theta}\). Test
\(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta > \theta_0\). The sum
\(T = \sum X_i\) is a sufficient statistic, and
\(T \sim \text{Gamma}(n, \theta)\).

The Likelihood Ratio for \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\) is:

\[
\frac{L(\theta_1)}{L(\theta_0)} = \frac{\theta_1^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_1}}{\theta_0^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_0}} = \left(\frac{\theta_0}{\theta_1}\right)^n \exp \left\{ \left( \frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1} \right) \sum x_i \right\}
\]

Since \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the term
\((\frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1})\) is positive. Thus,
\(\Lambda(x)\) is an increasing function of \(\sum x_i\). Rejecting for
large \(\Lambda(x)\) is equivalent to rejecting for \(\sum x_i > C\).

This test form does not depend on the specific \(\theta_1\), so it is
UMP for all \(\theta > \theta_0\).

\end{example}

\subsection{Note on Two-Sided
Hypotheses}\label{note-on-two-sided-hypotheses}

For testing \(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta \neq \theta_0\)
(e.g., in a Normal distribution), a UMP test generally \textbf{does not
exist}. This is because the ``best'' rejection region for
\(\theta > \theta_0\) (right tail) is completely different from the
``best'' region for \(\theta < \theta_0\) (left tail).
